هذا من مزالت الاقدام ولم تجد هذا الاسلوب في الشروح

ایک مثال سے احناف کے مسلک کی تائید

وصار كما اذا قطع العبد يمينيهما على التعاقب فتستحق رقبته لهما ترجمہ اور یہ ایسا ہو گیا جیسے غلام نے ان دونوں کے ہاتھ کانٹے ہوں یکے بعد دیگرے تو ان دونوں کے لئے اس کی اردن کا استحقاق ہو گیا۔

تشریح اس مثال سے مصنف حنفیہ کے مسلک کی تائید فرماتے ہیں کہتے ہیں اگر کسی غلام نے زید اور بکر کا ہاتھ حسب سابق کاٹ دیا تو یہاں بھی دونوں کا استحقاق غلام کی گردن میں ثابت ہو گا یعنی یہ غلام ان دونوں کو دیدیا جائے گا لہذا اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ یہاں ایک کے اسے حق کا ثبوت دوسرے کے حق کے ثبوت سے مانع نہیں ہے اسی طرح پہلے مسئلے میں بھی ہو گا۔
And if both hands were cut at once, then in that case a lottery is drawn; whose name comes out, his hand is cut in retaliation, and the other is given the blood money for his hand, because in the case of succession, the first had already become entitled to the cutter's hand, so the right of the second cannot be established therein, just as on the basis of this principle, after a pledged item is pawned to one, it cannot be pawned again to another. And if it is a simultaneous case, then since the cutter's one hand cannot be the substitute for both their hands, whose hand should be cut in exchange? In that, a lottery is drawn, then his hand is cut in exchange, and the other is given the blood money. This is Imam Shafi'i's proof.

Our proof and answer to Imam Shafi'i's analogy:

And for us, they are equal in the cause of entitlement, so they are equal in its ruling, like two creditors in an estate. And qisas is ownership of an action, established despite inconsistency; it does not appear except in the right of execution. As for the locus, it is free from ownership, so it does not prevent the establishment of the second, unlike rahn, because the right is established in the locus.

Translation: And our proof is that these two are equal in the cause of entitlement, so they will be equal in the ruling of the cause, like two creditors in an estate. And qisas is an ownership of an action that is established despite inconsistency; so this ownership will only appear in the right of execution. In any case, the locus is devoid of ownership, so it will not prevent the establishment of the second ownership. Unlike rahn, because the right of execution is established in the locus. Commentary: This is our proof, the gist of which is that since both Zaid and Bakr's hands are cut, both should get qisas, because when they are equal in cause, they will also be equal in ruling. And its method is what we have explained. Its exact example is that Zaid owes fifty rupees to Bakr, forty to Khalid, and forty to Sajid, and he borrowed these debts at different times. Now Zaid dies and leaves property, then all have equal rights in that estate; it will not be considered that the one whose debt was earlier gets prior right. Similarly, here also it will not be considered, rather due to equality in cause, equality in ruling will be established.

'And qisas is ownership of action...' From here, the author wants to say that analogizing this issue to the issue of rahn is an analogy with a difference.

See, in qisas, the guardian of qisas does not own the killer; rather, the guardian only gets the right to execute qisas, although he cannot own the killer because the killer is free. And the pledged item is owned, and in the owned, the mortgagee's right attaches to the pawned item's essence, although it is in terms of value. But in any case, his right is related to the locus, because the mortgagee will collect his right from the locus. So here, the meaning of inconsistency is that due to being a killer, he cannot become owned, so its requirement was that due to his becoming free, the guardian should not get the right of qisas either, but we gave the right of qisas despite the inconsistency. But the reality of qisas is merely making one the owner of an action; ownership of the locus and the person is not established therein. Therefore, this ownership of action will only work to the extent that the guardian can execute his right. As for the killer's person and the locus of qisas, the guardian has no ownership. When this principle is understood, then understand the second principle: that where someone's right is not established in the locus, only the ownership of action relates, then the accumulation of ownership of action is possible. And where the right relates to the locus, after one right is established, another's right cannot be established. Therefore, since rahn's relation is to the locus, for that reason the pawned item cannot be pawned again to another. And qisas's relation is to ownership of action, in which the establishment of the right of ownership does not prevent the establishment of another's right. Summary: Imam Shafi'i's analogizing qisas to the issue of rahn in the mentioned issue is an analogy with a difference (qiyas ma' al-fariq).

This is from the slippery steps of feet, and you will not find this style in the commentaries.

Support for the Hanafi school with an example:

And it becomes as if a slave cut the right hands of both of them successively, then his neck becomes entitled to both of them. Translation: And it becomes as if a slave cut both their hands one after the other, then both of them become entitled to his neck.

Commentary: With this example, the author supports the Hanafi school. He says, if a slave cut Zaid and Bakr's hand according to the previous situation, then here also both would have entitlement to the slave's neck, meaning this slave will be given to both of them. Hence, it is learned that here the establishment of one's right does not prevent the establishment of another's right; similarly in the first issue.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents a legal dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam Shafi'i regarding a case where a person cuts off the right hands of two individuals. Imam Shafi'i argues that only one hand can be cut in retaliation (qisas) because a single hand cannot compensate for two, so a lottery is drawn; the winner gets qisas and the loser gets blood money. He supports this by analogy to a pawned item: once pawned to one, it cannot be pawned to another, implying that the right of the first victim precludes the second's claim to qisas.

The Hanafi school responds with two arguments. First, both victims are equal in the cause of entitlement (both had their hands cut), so they should be equal in the ruling. Just as creditors with different debt timings share equally in the deceased's estate, here both victims share equally in the right to qisas. Second, the nature of qisas differs from rahn. Qisas is ownership of an action (malak al-fi'l), not ownership of a person or object. The right attaches only to the act of execution, not to the aggressor's person. Therefore, multiple rights can accumulate because the locus (the aggressor's hand) is free from prior ownership. In rahn, the right attaches to the object itself, preventing a second pledge. Thus, analogizing qisas to rahn is an analogy with a difference (qiyas ma' al-fariq).

The passage further supports the Hanafi position with an example: if a slave cuts both victims' hands, the slave's entire body (neck) is given to both victims jointly, showing that multiple claims can coexist without one preventing the other.

Key Terms
ملک الفعل (malak al-fi'l): Ownership of an action; the right to perform a specific act (here, qisas) without owning the person or object involved.
محل (mahall): Locus or subject matter; in qisas, the aggressor's limb (e.g., hand) where the retaliation is executed.
حق فی العین (haqq fi al-'ayn): A right in the object itself, such as the mortgagee's right in a pawned item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi text, why can multiple victims have the right to qisas on the same aggressor?
A) Because the Quran explicitly permits it.
B) Because qisas is ownership of an action, not of the limb itself.
C) Because the victims can take turns executing the punishment.
D) Because the aggressor's hand is divisible.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi concept of malak al-fi'l (ownership of action) is the key to understanding why multiple victims can have equal rights to qisas without conflict.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate
8 min
Qisas When One Victim Present and One Absent
Ruling: present victim gets qisas, absent gets half diya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when one of two victims of a single hand amputation is present and the other absent, and explain the legal reasoning based on the priority of established rights.

Core Idea
When two victims share a single locus of qisas, the present victim's execution exhausts it; the absent receives half diya.

This passage addresses a specific scenario in qisas where an aggressor cuts the right hands of two individuals, but only one victim is present at the time of judgment. The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling that balances the rights of both victims. Relying on the principle that a settled right (the present victim) takes precedence, and once fulfilled, the pending right of the absent victim converts to monetary compensation (half diya). Understanding this ruling clarifies how Islamic law handles multiple claims against a single bodily part.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two victims
One aggressor cuts the right hands of two victims. At the time of execution, only one victim is present. The present victim's right to qisas is established; the absent victim's right is pending because his presence is uncertain.
The ruling distinguishes this from when both are present (previous discussion).
2
Recognize the legal status of each right
The present victim's right is thabit (established) and executable immediately. The absent victim's right is mutaraddid (pending) because it depends on his future presence.
This difference is crucial: only an established right can be enforced at the moment.
3
Understand the effect of executing qisas
When the present victim carries out qisas (the aggressor's hand is cut), the locus of execution (the hand) is exhausted. It cannot be cut again.
This is a physical limitation: qisas can only be applied once to a limb.
4
Derive the conversion to diya
Since the hand is gone, the absent victim's right cannot be satisfied by qisas. It automatically converts to monetary compensation: half the diya for a hand (nisf al-diya).
The amount is half the full diya of a person because a hand is worth half the full diya (500 dinars or equivalent).
5
Apply the underlying principle
The reasoning is based on the principle that an established right takes precedence over a pending right. Once the established right is fulfilled, the pending right transforms into its monetary equivalent.
This principle appears elsewhere in Hanafi fiqh, e.g., in inheritance or debt priorities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان حضر واحد منهما فقطع يده فللأخر عليه نصف الدية لان للحاضر ان يستوفى لثبوت حقه وتردد حق الغائب واذا ستوفى لم يبق محل الاستيفاء فيتعين حق الاخر فى الدية لانه اوفی به حقا مستحقا ترجمہ اور اگر ان دونوں میں سے ایک حاضر ہوا پس اس نے اس کا ہاتھ کاٹ دیا تو دوسرے کے لئے اس پر آدھی دیت ہے اس لئے کہ حاضر کے لئے اپنا حق وصول کرنے کا حق ہے اس کا حق ثابت ہو جانے کی وجہ سے اور غائب کے حق میں تردد کی وجہ سے اور جب حاضر نے اپنا حق وصول کر لیا تو محل استیفاء باقی نہیں رہا تو دوسرے کا حق دیت کے اندر متعین ہو گیا اس لئے کہ جنایت کرنے والے نے اس کے ذریعہ اپنے اوپر ایک حق واجبی کو ادا کیا ہے۔ تشریح ماقبل میں جو مسئلہ بیان کیا گیا ہے یہ حکم اس وقت کا ہے کہ جن دونوں کے ہاتھ کاٹے گئے ہیں وہ دونوں حاضر ہو جائیں۔ اور اگر صرف ایک حاضر ہو اور دوسرا غا ئب ہے اس کا حکم یہ ہے کہ جو یہاں مذکور ہے یعنی حاضر اپنا قصاص وصول کرے اور جو غائب
And if one of them is present and cut his hand, then the other has half the blood money against him, because the present one has the right to execute due to the establishment of his right and the absent one's right is pending. And when he has executed, the place of execution does not remain, so the other's right is determined in the blood money, because the perpetrator has thereby discharged a necessary right upon himself. Translation: And if from among these two, one is present and cut his hand, then the other has half the blood money against him, because the present one has the right to take his right due to the establishment of his right, and the absent one's right is pending. And when the present has taken his right, the place of execution remains no more, so the other's right becomes fixed in blood money, because the perpetrator has thereby discharged an obligatory right upon himself. Commentary: In the previous issue, the ruling was for when both victims are present. And if only one is present and the other absent, the ruling is as mentioned here: the present executes qisas, and the absent gets half diya.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage gives a ruling for a specific scenario: one person cuts the right hands of two individuals, but only one victim is present at the time of judgment. The present victim has the right to execute qisas immediately because his right is established and certain. The absent victim's right is pending (mutaraddid) because it is not yet known whether he will be present or not. Once the present victim carries out the retaliation, the locus of execution (the aggressor's hand) is exhausted. Since qisas can only be done once, the absent victim's right converts to half the blood money (diya). The reason is that the aggressor has fully satisfied one obligatory right (the present victim's qisas), and the only way to compensate the absent victim is through monetary payment. This ruling distinguishes between the case where both are present (discussed earlier) and this case where one is absent.

Key Terms
نصف الدية (nisf al-diya): Half the standard blood money for a hand (which is half of full diya for a limb).
حق مستحق (haqq mustahaqq): An obligatory right that is established and due.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the absent victim get half diya instead of full?
A) Because his hand was only half cut
B) Because the present victim already got qisas, leaving only half the hand to compensate
C) Because the diya for a hand is half the full diya of a person
D) Because the absent victim's right was pending and becomes half through tawzee

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when one of two victims of a single mutilation is absent, the present victim gets qisas, and the absent victim receives half diya, based on the priority of established rights and exhaustion of the locus of execution.

Qisas as Right of Action: Refuting the Shafi'i Analogy
Hanafi refutation of Shafi'i's qiyas of qisas to rahn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that multiple qisas claims can coexist and refute the Shafi'i analogy using the distinction between malik al-fi'l and malik al-mahall.

Core Idea
Qisas is a right to an act, not ownership of the body; multiple rights to the same act can coexist.

This lesson delves into a foundational Hanafi principle: the distinction between ownership of an action (malik al-fi'l) and ownership of the subject (malik al-mahall). Using this distinction, the Hanafi school refutes Imam al-Shafi'i's analogy between qisas (retaliation) and rahn (pawn). Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the Hanafi position on multiple qisas claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key principle
The Hanafi principle that qisas is malik al-fi'l, not malik al-mahall. The guardian has only the right to perform the act of retaliation, not ownership of the offender's body.
This principle is the foundation of the Hanafi argument.
2
Examine the Shafi'i analogy
Imam al-Shafi'i analogizes qisas to rahn: just as a pawned item cannot be pawned again, qisas cannot be claimed by two persons.
The analogy is based on the assumption that both involve rights in a physical subject.
3
Analyze the Hanafi refutation
The refutation: rahn involves right in the subject (mahall), which cannot be shared, while qisas involves right in action (fi'l), which can aggregate. This is a qiyas ma'a al-fariq (flawed analogy due to differing underlying cause).
The distinction between malik al-fi'l and malik al-mahall is crucial.
4
Apply the supporting example
The example of a slave cutting off the hands of two persons: both are entitled to the slave's neck, demonstrating coexistence of rights. This supports the consistency of the Hanafi view.
The example reinforces that qisas rights can coexist even when multiple claims arise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قصاص بھی نہ ملے مگر ہم نے منافی کے باوجود حق قصاص دیا لیکن قصاص کی حقیقت صرف ایک فعل کا مالک بنانا ہے محل اور ذات کی ملکیت اس میں ثابت نہیں ہوگی ۔ لہذا یہ ملک فعل صرف یہاں تک کام کرے گا کہ ولی قصاص اپنا حق وصول کر سکتا ہے۔ رہی قاتل کی ذات اور محل قصاص اس میں ولی قصاص کی کوئی ملکیت نہیں ہے جب یہ اصول ذہن نشین ہو گیا تو دوسرا اصول یہ سمجھئے کہ جس جگہ کسی کا حق محل میں ثابت نہ ہو صرف ملک فعل سے اس کا تعلق ہو تو وہاں ملک فعل کا اجتماع ہو سکتا ہے۔ اور جہاں حق کا تعلق محل سے وہاں ایک حق کے ثبوت کے بعد دوسرے کا حق ثابت نہیں ہو سکتا۔ لہذا ر ہن کا تعلق چونکہ محل سے ہے اس وجہ سے اب اس مرہون کو دوسرے کے پاس رہن نہیں رکھا جا سکتا اور قصاص کا تعلق ملک فعل سے ہے۔ جس میں ملکیت کے حق کا ثبوت دوسرے کا حق ثابت ہونے سے نہیں روکتا۔ خلاصه کلام امام شافعی کا مذکورہ مسئلہ میں قصاص کو رہن کے مسئلہ پر قیاس کرنا قیاس مع الفارق ہے۔
هذا من مزالت الاقدام ولم تجد هذا الاسلوب في الشروح
ایک مثال سے احناف کے مسلک کی تائید
وصار كما اذا قطع العبد يمينيهما على التعاقب فتستحق رقبته لهما ترجمہ اور یہ ایسا ہو گیا جیسے غلام نے ان دونوں کے ہاتھ کانٹے ہوں یکے بعد دیگرے تو ان دونوں کے لئے اس کی اردن کا استحقاق ہو گیا۔
تشریح اس مثال سے مصنف حنفیہ کے مسلک کی تائید فرماتے ہیں کہتے ہیں اگر کسی غلام نے زید اور بکر کا ہاتھ حسب سابق کاٹ دیا تو یہاں بھی دونوں کا استحقاق غلام کی گردن میں ثابت ہو گا یعنی یہ غلام ان دونوں کو دیدیا جائے گا لہذا اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ یہاں ایک کے اسے حق کا ثبوت دوسرے کے حق کے ثبوت سے مانع نہیں ہے اسی طرح پہلے مسئلے میں بھی ہو گا۔
The meaning is: Despite the conflict, we gave the right of qisas. But the reality of qisas is only making one the owner of an action (malik al-fi'l); ownership of the subject (mahall) and of the person is not established in it. Therefore, this ownership of the action will only work to the extent that the guardian of qisas can collect his right. As for the killer's person and the subject of qisas, the guardian of qisas has no ownership in them. When this principle is understood, understand the second principle: In a place where someone's right is not established in the subject, but only related through ownership of the action, then multiple ownerships of action can coexist. And where the right is related to the subject, after the establishment of one right, another right cannot be established. Hence, because rahn (pawn) is related to the subject, that mortgaged item cannot be pawned again to another. And qisas is related to ownership of action, where the establishment of one right does not prevent the establishment of another right. In summary, Imam Shafi'i's analogy of qisas to rahn in the mentioned issue is an analogy with a difference (qiyas ma'a al-fariq). This is from the slippery slopes and you will not find this style in the commentaries. A supporting example for the Hanafi school: 'And it became like when a slave cuts off the right hands of two persons successively, then his neck becomes entitled to both of them.' Translation: And it became like when a slave cut off the right hands of both of them one after the other, then both of them become entitled to his neck. Commentary: With this example, the author supports the Hanafi school. He says: If a slave cut off the right hand of Zaid and Bakr successively, then here also both of them have entitlement established in the slave's neck – meaning this slave will be given to both of them. Hence, it is known that here the establishment of one right does not prevent the establishment of another right, and similarly in the earlier issue.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute passage where the Hanafi school refutes Imam Shafi'i's analogy between qisas and rahn. The author first establishes a key Hanafi principle: qisas is a right of action (malik al-fi'l), not a right in the subject (malik al-ayn or mahall). This means that multiple guardians of qisas can all have the right to exact the penalty from the same defendant, because each holds only the right to perform the act (cutting off the hand, etc.), not ownership of the defendant's body or the limb. In contrast, rahn involves ownership of the subject (the pawned item itself), so it cannot be shared. The author then strengthens the argument with a supporting example from the Hanafi tradition: if a slave cuts off the right hands of two people successively, both are entitled to the slave's neck (i.e., the slave is given to both as compensation), because each has a right to the act of taking the slave, not ownership of the slave as a commodity. This demonstrates the consistency of the Hanafi position. The passage concludes that Shafi'i's qiyas is 'qiyas ma'a al-fariq' – an analogy where the effective cause differs, and thus invalid.

Key Terms
ملک فعل (malik al-fi'l): Ownership of an action; the right to perform an act (like taking qisas) without ownership of the physical subject.
محل (mahall): The physical subject or object in which a right is vested; the location of the right.
قیاس مع الفارق (qiyas ma'a al-fariq): An analogy that is invalid because the effective cause ('illa) differs between the original case and the new case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because qisas and rahn are both rights in the subject (mahall).
B) Because qisas is a right of action (malik al-fi'l) while rahn is a right in the subject (malik al-mahall).
C) Because qisas is a financial right and rahn is not.
D) Because the guardian of qisas cannot collect his right.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes qisas as a right of action (malik al-fi'l) that can aggregate, overriding the Shafi'i analogy to rahn.

One Victim Present, One Absent: Qisas and Diyah
Ruling when one of two victims of qisas is present and the other absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when one of two qisas victims is absent and explain the legal reasoning why the absent victim's right converts to diyah.

Core Idea
When one victim takes qisas, the absent victim's right is not lost but converts to diyah.

In the previous lesson, we learned the ruling when both victims of qisas are present. Now we examine a more nuanced scenario: only one victim is present. The Hanafi school provides a clear ruling that protects the absent victim's right while allowing the present one to proceed without delay. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind this conversion from qisas to diyah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and both rights
Two victims had their right hands cut off by the same offender. One victim is present and demands qisas; the other is absent. Both have a right to qisas against the offender's hand.
2
Analyze the present victim's right
The present victim's right is established and certain (thabut haqq). He can exact qisas immediately because there is no obstacle from the absent victim's right.
The absentee's right does not delay the present one's due to uncertainty.
3
Analyze the absent victim's right
The absent victim's right is uncertain (taraddud) – he may forgive, not appear, or die. This uncertainty prevents his right from blocking the present victim's immediate exaction.
4
Consequence of exaction: exhaustion of locus
When the present victim exacts qisas by cutting off the offender's hand, the locus of exaction (mahall al-istifa') is exhausted. There is no other hand to cut for the absent victim.
5
Conversion to diyah
Since the right is not extinguished but the locus is gone, the absent victim's right converts to diyah (blood money). The cutter discharged a due right, but the other's right remains as a financial claim.
Half diyah of a hand is due, as per the principle that the right of the other is not lost.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان حضر واحد منهما فقطع يده فللأخر عليه نصف الدية لان للحاضر ان يستوفى لثبوت حقه وتردد حق الغائب واذا ستوفى لم يبق محل الاستيفاء فيتعين حق الاخر فى الدية لانه اوفی به حقا مستحقا ترجمہ اور اگر ان دونوں میں سے ایک حاضر ہوا پس اس نے اس کا ہاتھ کاٹ دیا تو دوسرے کے لئے اس پر آدھی دیت ہے اس لئے کہ حاضر کے لئے اپنا حق وصول کرنے کا حق ہے اس کا حق ثابت ہو جانے کی وجہ سے اور غائب کے حق میں تردد کی وجہ سے اور جب حاضر نے اپنا حق وصول کر لیا تو محل استیفاء باقی نہیں رہا تو دوسرے کا حق دیت کے اندر متعین ہو گیا اس لئے کہ جنایت کرنے والے نے اس کے ذریعہ اپنے اوپر ایک حق واجبی کو ادا کیا ہے۔ تشریح ماقبل میں جو مسئلہ بیان کیا گیا ہے یہ حکم اس وقت کا ہے کہ جن دونوں کے ہاتھ کاٹے گئے ہیں وہ دونوں حاضر ہو جائیں۔ اور اگر صرف ایک حاضر ہو اور دوسرا غا ئب ہے اس کا حکم یہ ہے جو یہاں مذکور ہے یعنی حاضر اپنا قصاص وصول کرے اور جو غائب ہے اس کیلئے نصف دیت واجب ہوگی کیونکہ ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک کا ہاتھ کٹا ہے تو وصولیابی کا حق ہر ایک کو حاصل ہے لہذا جو حاضر ہے اس کو حق ہوگا کہ وہ اپنا حق وصول کرے اور رہا غائب تو اس کی وجہ سے حاضر کا حق موخر کرنا ضروری نہ ہوگا کیونکہ غائب کے حق میں تو ابھی تردد ہے کیونکہ ہو سکتا ہے کہ وہ اپنا حق معاف کر دے اور ہو سکتا ہے کہ وہ حاضر ہی نہ ہو سکے۔
الہذا حاضر کو حق ملا کہ اپنا حق وصول کر لے تو جب اس نے وصول کر لیا تو چونکہ مثل استیفاء، یعنی داہنا ہاتھ ختم ہو گیا تو اب دوسرے کا حق ویت کے اندر متعین ہو گیا۔ کیوں؟ اس لیئے کہ قاطع ید نے اپنے ہاتھ سے اس حق کو ادا کیا ہے جو اس پر واجب تھا اور ایسی صورت میں صاحب حق ختم نہیں ہوا کرتا جس کی تفصیل ماقبل میں گذر چکی ہے۔
And if one of them is present and cuts off his hand, then for the other is half the diyah upon him, because the present one has the right to exact his right due to establishment of his right, and the absent one's right is uncertain. When the present one exacts, the locus of exaction (mahall al-istifa') no longer remains, so the other's right becomes determined in diyah, because the cutter has thereby discharged a right that was due upon him. Commentary: The earlier ruling was when both victims were present. If only one is present and the other absent, the present one takes his qisas, and for the absent one, half diyah is obligatory. Because each has a full right to his hand. The present one can exact his right, and the absent one's right does not delay the present one's right, as the absent one's right is uncertain (he may forgive or not appear). So the present one takes qisas; the hand is gone, so the absent one's right transforms into diyah. Why? Because the cutter has discharged a right that was due, and in such a case the right of the other is not extinguished; details earlier.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This is a ruling statement with an explanation of the legal reasoning. The scenario: Two individuals had their right hands cut off by the same offender. If only one victim is present, the present victim may immediately exact qisas from the offender's right hand. Since the offender's right hand is then cut off, the locus of exaction (hand) no longer exists for the absent victim. Therefore, the absent victim's right transforms into a financial right – half diyah of the hand. The reasoning is based on ta'addud (multiplicity) of rights and uncertainty of the absent party's claim. The present victim's right is established and certain, while the absent victim's is uncertain (he might forgive, die, or fail to appear). Hence, the present one's right takes precedence in execution. Once executed, the absent one's right converts to diyah. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that qisas and diyah can coexist as alternatives depending on circumstances.

Key Terms
محل استیفاء (mahall al-istifa'): The subject or organ upon which the right of qisas is exacted (e.g., the right hand of the offender).
تردد (taraddud): Uncertainty; here, the absent victim's right is uncertain because he may not claim or may forgive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is extinguished entirely.
B) It converts to half diyah.
C) It remains as qisas to be exacted later.
D) It is transferred to the state.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when one of two qisas victims is absent, the present victim may take qisas immediately, and the absent victim's right is preserved through conversion to diyah.

Qisas — Retaliation

Validity of a Slave's Confession to Intentional Murder
Dispute over slave's confession to murder

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that a slave's confession to intentional murder is valid by distinguishing it from financial confession using the concept of mutahham.

Core Idea
A slave's confession to murder is valid because he risks his own life, not the master's property.

This lesson examines a dispute within the Hanafi school: does a slave's confession to intentional murder bind him to qisas? Imam Zufar argued that it does not, analogizing it to financial confessions, which harm the master. The majority Hanafi position responds by highlighting a crucial difference: in murder, the slave faces execution, removing any suspicion of collusion. We will unpack the arguments and understand how the madhhab prioritizes human life over property.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position from the matn
Identify the ruling in the text: when a slave confesses to intentional murder, qisas becomes binding. Note that this is the position of Quduri and the majority.
2
Identify Zufar's objection and its basis
Zufar argues via qiyas: a slave's confession to a debt is invalid because it harms the master. Likewise, his confession to murder, which also harms the master (by destroying property), should be invalid. Understand the logic of analogy.
Zufar is a Hanafi imam, so this is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response – two proofs
First, the slave is not mutahham (accused of dishonesty) because the confession harms himself, not the master. Second, in blood matters, the slave retains original freedom (asl al-hurriyyah) due to his humanity. These neutralize the analogy.
The term mutahham is key: it refers to suspicion of collusion. In murder, self-harm negates suspicion.
4
Understand the principle 'the incidental is overlooked'
The nullification of the master's property right is incidental (tābiʿī), not the primary effect. In Islamic jurisprudence, incidental harms are tolerated when the primary objective is valid.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Under Zufar's view, the slave is not executed; the master may claim diyah or pardon. Under the Hanafi view, the slave is executed, and the master loses his property value. Understand the tangible legal consequence.
This shows how a procedural rule (iqrar validity) affects substantive criminal law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال واذا اقر العبد بقتل العمد لزمه القود وقال زفر لا يصح اقراره لانه يلا قى حق المولى بالابطال فصار كما اذا اقر بالمال ولنا انه غير متهم فيه لانه مضربه فيقبل ولان العبد مبقى على اصل الحرية في حق الدم عملا بالادمية حتى لا يصح اقرار المولى عليه بالحدود والقصاص وبطلان حق المولى بطريق الضمن فلا يبالى به ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور جب کہ غلام نے قتل عمد کا اقرار کیا تو اس پر قصاص لازم ہو گا اور زفر نے فرمایا کہ اس کا اقرار صحیح نہ ہو گا اس لئے کہ اس کا اقرار ملاقی ہوا ہے حق مولیٰ سے باطل کرنے کے ساتھ تو ایسا ہو گیا جب کہ غلام نے مال کا اقرار کیا ہو اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ غلام اس میں متہم نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے کہ اقرار غلام کیلئے مضر ہے تو اس کا اقرار قبول کر لیا جائے گا اور اس لئے کہ غلام خون کے حق میں اصل حریت پر باقی ہے آدمیت پر عمل کرتے ہوئے یہاں تک کہ علائم آقا کا حدود و قصاص کا اقرار صیح نہ ہوگا اور آقا کے حق کا بطلان ضمناً ہے تو اس کی پر اوہ نہیں کی جائے گی۔
تشریح غلام قتل عمد کا اقرار کرتا ہے تو اس کا اقرار معتبر ہوگا یا نہیں اس میں امام زفر کا قول یہ ہے کہ اگر اس کا اقرار معتبر ہو جائے تو غلام کو قصاص میں قتل کر دیا جائے گا اور مولیٰ کا حق باطل ہو جائے گا حالانکہ غلام جب ایسا اقرار کرے جس میں آقا کا حق باطل ہوتا ہو تو وہاں اس کا اقرار معتبر نہیں ہوتا۔ مثلاً غلام نے اقرار کیا کہ فلاں کے میرے ذمہ اتنے روپے ہیں تو یہ اقرار معتبر نہ ہو گا اسی طرح قتل عمد کا اقرار بھی درست نہ ہوگا۔
ہمارا مذ ہب یہ ہے کہ اقرار سیح ہے اور غلام سے قصاص لیا جائے گا۔ رہا آقا کے حق کا بطلان تو وہ ضمنی چیز ہے مقصود اصلی نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ اس اقرار میں غلام پر کوئی تہمت نہیں اور مال کے اقرار میں تہمت ہے کیونکہ آقا سے زیادہ نقصان تو خود غلام کا ہے کہ اس کی جان جا رہی ہے لہذا اس کا اقرار مقبول ہو گا۔
پھر اگر آقا اپنے غلام پر حدود یا قصاص کا اقرار کرے تو اس کا اقرار صحیح نہ ہوگا اور مولی کے اقرار کی وجہ سے غلام پر حد یا قصاص جاری نہ ہو گا تو اس مسئلہ سے یہ بات معلوم ہوگئی کہ غلام اگر چہ غلام اور مملوک ہے لیکن اس کی آدمیت اور انسانیت کا لحاظ کرتے ہوئے یہ قاعدہ
He (Quduri) said: When a slave confesses to intentional murder, qisas becomes binding upon him. Zufar said: His confession is not valid because it conflicts with the master's right by invalidating it, so it is like when he confesses to a debt. Our proof: He is not accused (mutahham) in this because it is harmful to him, so it is accepted. And because the slave remains on the original freedom regarding blood rights, operating on his humanity (adamiyyah), to the extent that the master's confession of hadd or qisas against him is not valid. And the invalidation of the master's right is incidental, so it is not considered. Commentary: Whether a slave's confession to intentional murder is valid. Imam Zufar says if it were valid, the slave would be killed and the master's right nullified. But whenever a slave makes a confession that nullifies the master's right, it is not valid – e.g., if he confesses to a debt, it is not accepted. Similarly, confession to murder should not be valid. Our madhhab: The confession is valid and qisas will be taken. The nullification of the master's right is incidental, not the main objective. Because the slave is not accused (mutahham) in this confession; indeed, it is harmful to his own life. In the case of debt, he might collude with the claimant to harm the master, but here he is endangering his own life. Therefore his confession is accepted. Furthermore, if a master confesses to hadd or qisas against his own slave, that confession is not valid and the hadd or qisas will not be executed. From this it is learned that although a slave is property, consideration of his humanity (adamiyyah) is given in matters of blood.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute (though Zufar is a Hanafi imam) regarding the validity of a slave's confession to intentional murder. The matn from Quduri states the Hanafi position: the confession is valid and qisas is binding. Zufar argues by analogy to financial confession (iqrar bil-mal), which is not accepted from a slave because it harms the master. The Hanafi response: The two cases are different. In financial confession, the slave may be colluding with the claimant to take the master's property (hence accusation of dishonesty). In qisas, the slave is confessing to an act that will cost him his own life – there is no presumption of collusion; rather, the confession is against his own interest. Additionally, the Hanafi principle is that in blood matters, the slave retains his original freedom (asl al-hurriyyah) in legal capacity. This is why a master's confession to a crime against his slave is not accepted – because the slave's humanity is considered. The nullification of the master's property right (since killing the slave destroys his value) is incidental and does not invalidate the confession. Therefore, the relied-upon Hanafi position is that the slave's confession to murder is valid and qisas is carried out. The passage ends with a broader principle: despite being property, a slave's humanity is considered in blood crimes.

Key Terms
متہم (mutahham): Accused of dishonesty or collusion; a person whose statement is suspect because it may be biased.
اصل حریت (asl al-hurriyyah): Original freedom: the legal presumption that a person is free unless proven otherwise; in blood matters, even a slave is treated as free in terms of capacity to be liable for qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, why does a slave's confession to intentional murder differ from his confession to a debt?
A) Because murder is a greater sin.
B) Because in murder the slave is not accused (mutahham), while in debt he may collude with the claimant.
C) Because the master allows it.
D) Because the diyah is less than the debt.

Key Takeaway
A slave's confession to intentional murder is valid in the Hanafi school because the slave is not mutahham (suspected of collusion) and his humanity is recognized in blood matters, making the master's property loss incidental.
